Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c04513/asset/images/medium/am0c04513_0006.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c04513/asset/images/large/am0c04513_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c04513&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">An engaging area of research in sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) has been focusing on discovery, design, and synthesis of high-capacity cathode materials in order to boost energy density to levels close enough to that of state-of-the-art lithium-ion batteries. Of particular interest, P2-type layered oxide, Na<sub>2/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, has been researched as a potential cathode in SIBs based on its high theoretical capacity of 260 mA h/g and use of noncritical materials. However, the reported synthesis methods are not only complex and energy-demanding but also often yield inhomogeneous and impure materials with capacities less than 200 mA h/g under impractical test conditions. Here, we report a novel synthesis route using low-temperature eutectic reaction to produce highly homogeneous, crystalline, and impurity-free P2-Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> with enhanced Na-ion diffusivity and kinetics. The overall electrochemical performances of the Na-ion cells have been improved by pairing the P2-cathode with presodiated hard carbon anodes, leading to reversible capacities in the range of 180 mA h/g. This new approach is a contribution toward the simplification of synthesis and scalability of sodium-based cathodes with high crystallinity and fine-tuned morphology and the realization of a sodium-ion battery system with lower cost and improved electrochemical performance.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D23951%26pageCount%3D8%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DMengya%2BLi%252C%2BDavid%2BL.%2BWood%252C%2BYaocai%2BBai%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D12%26issueNum%3D21%26contentID%3Dacsami.0c04513%26title%3DEutectic%2BSynthesis%2Bof%2Bthe%2BP2-Type%2BNaxFe1%252F2Mn1%252F2O2%2BCathode%2Bwith%2BImproved%2BCell%2BDesign%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D8%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D23958%26publicationDate%3DMay%2B2020" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2020 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291952&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrochemical cells"> Electrochemical cells </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292506&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Materials"> Materials </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290797&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Metals"> Metals </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292316&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Transition metals"> Transition metals </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Na2%2F3fe1%2F2mn1%2F2o2&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Na2%2F3Fe1%2F2Mn1%2F2O2">Na<sub>2/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub></a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Eutectic+Alloy+Synthesis&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_eutectic%20alloy%20synthesis">eutectic alloy synthesis</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sol%E2%88%92gel+Synthesis&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sol%E2%88%92gel%20synthesis">sol−gel synthesis</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Hard+Carbon&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_hard%20carbon">hard carbon</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodiation&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodiation">sodiation</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">With the 25% annual growth foreseen for Li-ion batteries (LIBs), the demand for cobalt- and nickel-based cathodes will continue to rise because of the high energy density of these cathodes and their improved cycling against carbonaceous anodes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1,2)</a> However, the high cost of cobalt and nickel and their geographical availability and market fluctuations have become major concerns voiced by research and industry communities. In addition, besides cathodes, other components such as the electrolytes, copper current collector, and separator and the use of energy-intensive manufacturing significantly add to the cost structure of LIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3" aria-label="reference 3">(3)</a> Therefore, the search for lower-cost energy-storage systems with less dependence on critical materials has been recently one of the major challenges for the research community working on batteries for transportation and stationary energy-storage applications. Among other options, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) stand out well because of everlasting availability of sodium as opposed to lithium in the earth’s crust. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 4 and 5">(4,5)</a> Previous reports laid the ground for the cost advantage of SIBs over LIBs based on cost reductions of copper and use of cobalt- and nickel-free cathodes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 6 to 10">(6−10)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Nevertheless, despite the above advantages, several challenges in SIBs are yet to be addressed including their lower energy density and unsatisfactory cycling life in full-cell configurations. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 11 and 12">(11,12)</a> In a typical sodium-ion battery, transition-metal oxide (TMO) cathodes and hard carbon anodes have been extensively studied as viable electrochemical couples to achieve conclusive performance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13 ref14" aria-label="reference 13 and 14">(13,14)</a> Of significance, layered structure P2- or O3-type cathodes have attracted much interest because of their peculiar crystalline orders ensured by the ABBAAB (P2) or ABCABC (O3) stacking along the crystalline <i>c</i>-axis, where Na ions are located in prismatic or octahedral sites between the TMO-layered slabs. When compared to O3-type cathodes, P2-type cathodes have the following advantages: (a) faster ion diffusion between neighboring prismatic sites through direct face-sharing facets, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 15 and 16">(15,16)</a> (b) higher phase transition reversibility during sodium removal and uptake, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17 ref18" aria-label="reference 17 and 18">(17,18)</a> and (c) higher reversible capacities in sodium cells. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 19 and 20">(19,20)</a> The potential of P2-type cathode was also shown when the material was paired with hard carbon in SIB cells, where hard carbon yielded reversible capacities of over 250 mA h/g. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13 ref21" aria-label="reference 13 and 21">(13,21)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">Despite the promise, conventional syntheses of P2-type cathodes such as solid-state or sol–gel had often led to impure and inhomogeneous phase formation. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 22 and 23">(22,23)</a> These synthesis methods are not only nonscalable and impractical, but they also lead to unsatisfactory electrochemical performance in the case of P2-cathodes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 24 and 25">(24,25)</a> In this work, we used a novel approach based on a room temperature eutectic synthesis route to produce highly pure P2-type cathode Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> with improved crystallinity and morphology. A reversible capacity of 188 mA h/g was achieved in a half-cell configuration against the Na metal. Enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics has been realized by this new synthesis approach in comparison with the sol–gel method. To further improve its cycle life, we paired the P2-Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode with presodiated hard carbon anodes, which resulted in 182 mAh/g reversible capacity and improved capacity retention over 50 cycles. With this presodiation approach of the hard carbon anode, we demonstrated that cells made of sodium-deficient P2-cathodes can maintain a high capacity and a good cycle life. Our study paves the way toward the realization of scalable synthesis of P2-cathodes with enhanced surface and bulk Na<sup>+</sup>-storage properties and sheds light on the future design of practical and long-life SIBs.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Material Synthesis and Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p">Sodium hydroxide (NaOH), iron nitrate nonahydrate [Fe(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·9H<sub>2</sub>O], and manganese nitrate tetrahydrate [Mn(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·4H<sub>2</sub>O], all from Sigma-Aldrich, were used as precursors for the synthesis of Na<sub>2/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. Sol–gel synthesis was performed by dissolving 8 g of Fe(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·9H<sub>2</sub>O and 5 g of Mn(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·4H<sub>2</sub>O in 300 mL of deionized water preheated at 100 °C in a beaker under continuous stirring. NaOH solution was then added to the mixture, followed by overnight drying at 120 °C. The dry mixture was ground before precalcination at 400 °C for 6 h to remove NO<sub>2</sub>, followed by annealing at 950 °C for 12 h.</div><div class="NLM_p">Eutectic alloy synthesis was performed by mixing together appropriate amounts of the above precursors in a mortar. Thereafter, the solid mix turned into uniform dark-brown liquid and was dried overnight at 120 °C. The resulting dry mix was then precalcined at 400 °C, followed by a final calcination at 950 °C for 12 h.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Morphology of the synthesized materials was characterized by scanning electron microscopy (SEM) (Zeiss Merlin), and the crystal data were collected on an X-ray diffractometer (X’pert Pro PANalytical). Profile matching refinements for both the patterns were performed using the Jana2006 program package. Inductively coupled plasma (ICP)-optical emission spectroscopy was performed with an Optima 2100 DV spectrometer (PerkinElmer) to determine the chemical composition of the synthesized Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> materials.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.2.  Electrochemical Testing</h3><div class="NLM_p">Positive electrodes were fabricated by mixing 80 wt % Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> powders, 10 wt % polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) (Solvay 5130), and 10 wt % carbon black (Super C65) in the <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone (NMP) solvent. The slurry was then cast onto an aluminum foil and dried overnight at 115 °C inside a dry room (0.1% relative humidity with a dew point of less than −50 °C). CR2032 coin cells were assembled with 14 mm disks of electrodes. Sodium metal was used as the counter electrode with a glass fiber separator (Whatman GF/F). The electrolyte was 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> dissolved in propylene carbonate (PC) (Sigma-Aldrich) added to 5 vol % fluoroethylene carbonate (Sigma-Aldrich). Half-cells were tested between 1.5 and 4.3 V at 0.1 C (13 mA/g) for cycling and at various rates up to 5 C for rate performance evaluation.</div><div class="NLM_p">Negative electrodes were fabricated by mixing 92 wt % hard carbon powder (Kuranode, Kuraray), 6 wt % PVDF (Kureha 9300), and 2 wt % carbon black (Super C65) in NMP and then casted onto a copper foil. Punched hard carbon electrodes (15 mm disk, active material loading: 2.0–2.2 mg/cm<sup>2</sup>) were presodiated in sodium metal cells that were discharged and charged between 0.01 and 3 V at the current density of 25 mA/g to determine the 100% state of discharge, followed by discharging to various state of discharge (0, 50, and 100% depth of discharge). The cells were then disassembled, and the hard carbon anodes were washed with PC and dried inside a glovebox before being assembled into full-cells with the Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathodes (14 mm disk, active material loading: 2.2–2.4 mg/cm<sup>2</sup>, N/P ratio ranging from 1.3 to 1.5). Full-cells were cycled between 1.5 and 4.2 V at the current density of 13 mA/g<sub>cathode</sub>.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Sodium half-cells with the P2-cathode went through the first cycle between 1.5 and 4.3 V before being subjected to a test by the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT). GITT was performed by (1) charging the cells for 2 h to a certain state of charge, followed by (2) a 3 h relaxation period. Steps (1) and (2) were repeated loop wise till the cell reached the upper cutoff voltage of 4.3 V. A similar test profile was used during the discharge of the cell to 1.5 V. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was performed on coin cells at the beginning of the charge/discharge process and at the end of each loop after relaxing the cell for 3 h in the frequency range from 200 kHz to 0.1 Hz using a sinusoidal voltage amplitude of 10 mV. The obtained EIS spectra were fitted using an equivalent circuit model built in ZView software in order to extract resistance values associated with the electrochemical processes. Charge-transfer resistances are extracted from the first and second semicircles in our simulation of the Nyquist plots.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i6">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The morphologies of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> synthesized by the sol–gel method (labeled SG-P2 thereafter) and the eutectic alloy method (labeled EA-P2 thereafter) are compared in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>. Sol–gel synthesis mostly produced large particles over 10–15 μm composed of hexagonal plates and aggregates (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a,b). In contrast, Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, synthesized by the eutectic alloy method, exhibited much smaller platelets in the range of 1–2 μm within a porous microstructure (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c,d). X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns of the SG-P2 and EA-P2 samples are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>e. For both the synthesis methods, P2-type phase Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> can be identified with all major diffraction lines matching the peaks of P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> in reference (ICSD 01-083-7324). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> Profile matching refinements were performed (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c04513/suppl_file/am0c04513_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>), and the associated crystallography results indicated minimal differences between SG-P2 and EA-P2 (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>e). However, there were some impurities in the SG-P2 sample (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>e), such as NaMnO<sub>2</sub> and NaFeO<sub>2</sub>, which are due to a phenomenon known as atom-level segregation during the gelation process of mixed precursors. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> In contrast, the eutectic alloy method first involved a liquification process that enabled uniform and homogenous mixing on the atomic level, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> which yielded a highly pure and more crystalline phase. ICP results revealed that the “<i>x</i>” values were approximately 0.7 and 0.6 for SG-P2 and EA-P2, respectively, which indicate that the phases formed were sodium-deficient phases as expected for P2-type sodium-based cathodes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29 ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29 ref30" aria-label="reference 29 and 30">(29,30)</a></div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c04513/asset/images/medium/am0c04513_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c04513/asset/images/large/am0c04513_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. SEM images of (a,b) SG-P2 and (c,d) EA-P2 and e) XRD results of SG-P2 and EA-P2 Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (the circled peaks indicate impurities).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c04513/asset/images/large/am0c04513_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c04513&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical charge/discharge and cycling of the SG-P2 and EA-P2 compounds against the sodium metal anode are presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>. As can be seen in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a, SG-P2 Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> delivered an initial reversible capacity of 173 mA h/g. Upon charging, three major voltage regions were observed and assigned to the following redox activities, in agreement with the literature: (1) Mn<sup>3+/4+</sup> redox activity below 3.0 V, (2) Fe<sup>3+/4+</sup> redox activity between 3.0 and 4.0 V, and (3) oxygen anion redox reaction above 4.0 V. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref31 ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18 ref31 ref32" aria-label="reference 18, 31 and 32">(18,31,32)</a> In comparison, EA-P2 Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> exhibited higher initial reversible capacity of 188 mA h/g because of an improved Mn<sup>3+/4+</sup> redox process (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b). Moreover, EA-P2 exhibited a reduced voltage hysteresis compared to SG-P2 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c04513/suppl_file/am0c04513_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2a</a>), which could be attributed to the improved crystalline phase and smaller particle sizes of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> synthesized by the eutectic method. The cycling performances against the sodium metal of SG-P2 and EA-P2 are compared in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c. Both EA-P2 and SG-P2 retained about 150 mA h/g capacity after completion of 20 cycles, and the Coulombic efficiency remained ∼99% for both cathodes after the first cycle (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c04513/suppl_file/am0c04513_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2b</a>). The observed capacity decay of the P2-Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode can be attributed to phase transition-driven structural distortion that happens above 4.0 V. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> The slightly faster decay of EA-P2 compared to SG-P2 could be due to the high surface area of EA-P2 with smaller particle sizes that leads to more surface degradation when in contact with the electrolyte. The similar trends of capacity retention in sodium cells for both EA-P2 and SG-P2 can be attributed to the following reasons: (1) high reactivity of the sodium metal with the electrolyte upon cycling and/or (2) structural distortion and phase transitions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 33 and 34">(33,34)</a> These two possible reasons, especially, sodium metal reactivity, could have contributed to altering the benefit of the higher initial capacity obtained in the case of the EA-P2 material. Therefore, we believe that coupling these materials against realistic anodes such as hard carbon would be a better indicator on their cycling performances.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c04513/asset/images/medium/am0c04513_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c04513/asset/images/large/am0c04513_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of (a) SG-P2 and (b) EA-P2 Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathodes in a cell against the Na metal and (c) cycling performance comparison of SG-P2 and EA-P2 Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> at 13 mA/g between 1.5 and 4.3 V.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c04513/asset/images/large/am0c04513_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c04513&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Thereafter, we performed kinetics studies using GITT and EIS to measure the Na-ion diffusion values for the Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> materials prepared by sol–gel and eutectic methods. The details of the GITT and EIS measurements can be found in the experimental part and in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c04513/suppl_file/am0c04513_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3a,b</a>. Na-ion diffusion coefficients were calculated using the following <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="1">1</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c04513/asset/images/medium/am0c04513_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i7"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span>where (<i>L</i>), the diffusion length, was averaged from cross-sectional SEM images and the relaxation time (τ) was derived from the fitting of the voltage depolarization using the following <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="2">2</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c04513/asset/images/medium/am0c04513_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i8"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></span>where <i>A</i> is the constant term for a particular material and <i>U</i><sub>(<i>t</i>)</sub> and <i>U</i><sub>(<i>t</i>=∞)</sub> are the cell voltages at times <i>t</i> and <i>t</i><sub>(∞)</sub>. The slope of the plot, ln(<i>U</i><sub>(<i>t</i>)</sub> – <i>U</i><sub>(<i>t</i>=∞)</sub>) versus <i>t</i>, provides the relaxation time, τ. Details on this equation and its use can be found in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c04513/suppl_file/am0c04513_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3c,d</a> and in refs <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35 ref36 ref37 ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35 ref36 ref37 ref38" aria-label="reference 35 to 38">(35−38)</a> As can be seen in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a,b, the diffusion kinetics for Na<sup>+</sup> in the P2 material were slightly improved by the eutectic alloy method in comparison with the sol–gel method, with an average <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> of 1.83 × 10<sup>–13</sup> and 1.60 × 10<sup>–13</sup> cm<sup>2</sup>/s upon charge and discharge, respectively. The average diffusion coefficient values for the SG-P2 material were 1.61 × 10<sup>–13</sup> cm<sup>2</sup>/s (<i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> charge) and 1.56 × 10<sup>–13</sup> cm<sup>2</sup>/s (<i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> discharge). The overall improved Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics in the case of EA-P2 material can be attributed to improved crystallinity, uniform diffusion length, and absence of impurities. This observation is also consistent with the improved capacities associated with the Mn<sup>3+/4+</sup> and Fe<sup>3+/4+</sup> redox couples between 1.5 and 4 V in the case of EA-P2 Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. At the end of charge/discharge, a slight decrease of <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> was observed, which could be due to the sliding of MnO<sub>2</sub> slabs that leads to octahedral Na<sup>+</sup> vacancies as reported by others. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a></div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c04513/asset/images/medium/am0c04513_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c04513/asset/images/large/am0c04513_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient upon (a) second charge and (b) second discharge process for SG-P2 and EA-P2 Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathodes at different SOCs. (c) Schematic illustration of the interfaces within the P2-Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> half-cell configuration and Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion path upon charging. (d) Solution resistance (<i>R</i><sub>s</sub>) and (e) charge-transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct1</sub>) at the electrolyte/Na metal interface and (f) charge-transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct2</sub>) at the cathode/electrolyte interface of SG-P2 and EA-P2 Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathodes.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c04513/asset/images/large/am0c04513_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c04513&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">A schematic illustration of the interfaces within the cell configuration and corresponding resistances is provided in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c.&nbsp;The measured impedance spectra of the SG-P2 and EA-P2 samples consist of two semicircles in the medium frequency range followed by Warburg impedance spikes in the lower frequency range (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c04513/suppl_file/am0c04513_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4a</a>). The high-frequency intercept is due to the ohmic resistance of the liquid electrolyte along with a minor contribution of the solid electrolyte interface (SEI). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> The obtained impedance spectra were fitted with the equivalent circuit shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c04513/suppl_file/am0c04513_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4b</a>. The obtained resistances are compared in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d–f at different states of charge (SOCs). The ohmic resistances (<i>R</i><sub>s</sub>) for each cell remained approximately the same as a function of the SOCs (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d). However, the sample SG-P2 exhibited higher <i>R</i><sub>s</sub> (∼20 Ω) compared to the sample EA-P2 (∼4 Ω), even though we used the same electrolyte for these two cathodes. The difference in <i>R</i><sub>s</sub> values for SG-P2 and EA-P2 cathodes could be attributed to the (1) microstructure and larger particles of SG-P2 in the composite electrode leading to higher tortuosity in comparison with the EA-P2 composite electrode <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> and (2) reaction between electrolyte and impurities leading to a more ionically resistive SEI layer. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42 ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42 ref43" aria-label="reference 42 and 43">(42,43)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The charge-transfer resistance at the electrolyte/Na metal interface (<i>R</i><sub>ct1</sub>) is influenced by SEI formation on the metal anode surface. Upon charging/discharging, the SEI layer becomes more resistive because of electrolyte decomposition. Thus, <i>R</i><sub>ct1</sub> values for the SG-P2 cathode continuously increased within charge and discharge (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e). However, <i>R</i><sub>ct1</sub> for EA-P2 was higher initially but continuously decreased upon charging and discharging. This phenomenon indicates that a more reversible SEI can be formed at the electrolyte/Na metal anode for EA-P2 compared to SG-P2. However, it could also be due to the nature of the Na metal surfaces as these can be affected by the manual rolling of the metal. As for the charge-transfer resistances at the cathode/electrolyte interface (<i>R</i><sub>ct2</sub>), we noticed that the resistance values for both electrodes (SG-P2 and EA-P2) gradually decreased upon sodiation because of the formation of a mixed valence state, which enhanced the electronic conductivity of active particles and reduced the interfacial resistance (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>f). The observed differences of <i>R</i><sub>ct2</sub> between SG-P2 and EA-P2 might also be induced by the presence of impurities within the SG-P2 cathode. It is also possible that the smaller particle sizes of EA-P2 (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c,d) have contributed to lowering of <i>R</i><sub>ct2</sub> over the whole range of charge/discharge. Moreover, <i>R</i><sub>ct2</sub> for EA-P2 exhibited minimal changes in the charge/discharge cycle (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>f). However, <i>R</i><sub>ct2</sub> values for SG-P2 were higher during discharge than charge, which indicates that a resistive interface might have formed for the SG-P2 electrode within a cycle. In addition, the larger particle sizes of SG-P2 can result in less active electrochemical interface compared to EA-P2, which can further lead to higher interfacial resistance in the case of SG-P2. Postmortem analysis has also been performed through ex situ SEM (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c04513/suppl_file/am0c04513_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5a–c</a>). SG-P2 with bigger particle sizes developed cross-sectional cracks after cycling (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c04513/suppl_file/am0c04513_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5a</a>). In comparison, EA-P2 with smaller particle sizes in both half-cell (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c04513/suppl_file/am0c04513_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5b</a>) and full-cell (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c04513/suppl_file/am0c04513_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5c</a>) configurations maintained structural integrity after cycling. We also calculated the average Mn/Fe atomic ratio for SG-P2 and EA-P2 obtained from energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) elemental mapping results in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c04513/suppl_file/am0c04513_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>. The Fe in SG-P2 is more likely to be exhausted by being leached out in the electrolyte, which ultimately results in less reversible redox reactions, continuous pulverization, and poor cycle life compared to EA-P2, as can be confirmed by the charge/discharge curves and the corresponding d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> profiles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c04513/suppl_file/am0c04513_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5d–i</a>). It is worth noting that that the rate performance of the EA-P2 cathode in the SIB half-cell outperformed that of the SG-P2 cathode (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c04513/suppl_file/am0c04513_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>), which can also be attributed to the improved charge transfer and reversible redox reactions due to the smaller particle sizes and the homogeneous morphology of EA-P2.</div><div class="NLM_p">Previous report taught that the sodium metal can react with organic solvents in electrolytes leading to surface contamination that deteriorates the cell life. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> Therefore, it is important to evaluate sodium-metal-free cells to assess the cycling performance of new cathode materials. In our case, we used hard carbon, which we first tested against the sodium metal anode, and the results showed that hard carbon generated 250 mA h/g reversible capacity (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c04513/suppl_file/am0c04513_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>). We then fabricated cells comprising EA-P2 cathode and hard carbon anode. The cycling of the cells that contained nonsodiated hard carbon anodes resulted in a poor reversible capacity of only 80 mA h/g (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a). However, by electrochemically presodiating hard carbon to 50% depth of discharge (DOD), the cell displayed improved capacity (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b). In this case, a reversible capacity of 163 mA h/g was achieved, which is twofold higher than that of the cell assembled against pristine hard carbon (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b). Furthermore, we increased the presodiation level of HC to 100%. The cell was first discharged to fill in the Na<sup>+</sup> vacancies in the P2-type layered cathodes. In this case, a capacity of 55 mA h/g was achieved during the first discharge (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c). On charge and thereafter, the cell reversible capacity increased to 182 mA h/g. Distinct voltage plateaus corresponding to Mn<sup>3+/4+</sup> and Fe<sup>3+/4+</sup> redox couples were observed as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c. This cell configuration guaranteed enough Na<sup>+</sup> reservoir in the hard carbon to supply the Na-deficient P2 cathode, which had led to improved reversible Na<sup>+</sup> transport between the P2-Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode and the hard carbon anode. The Na-metal-free full-cell configuration with enough Na<sup>+</sup> reservoir on the anode side also minimized the degradation of the electrolyte and thus improved the capacity retention during cycling when compared to the half-cell configuration. The energy density for SIBs was calculated using the following <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="eq 3">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="3">3</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq3"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c04513/asset/images/medium/am0c04513_m003.gif" alt="" id="_i9"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(3)</span></div></div></span></div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c04513/asset/images/medium/am0c04513_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c04513/asset/images/large/am0c04513_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of cells made of EA-P2 cathode and hard carbon anode with presodiation levels of (a) 0% DOD, (b) 50% DOD, and (c) 100% DOD.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c04513/asset/images/large/am0c04513_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c04513&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The SIB that is composed of P2 cathode and hard carbon anode with 100% DOD resulted in a specific energy density of 203 W h/kg.</div><div class="NLM_p">Cycling performances of P2-Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>/HC cells are compared in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a. In the case of nonsodiated hard carbon, the cell exhibited faster capacity fading with only 26% capacity retention over 50 cycles, which can be attributed to both lack of enough Na<sup>+</sup> to compensate the vacancies in P2-type cathodes and continuous consumption of Na<sup>+</sup> upon cycling. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> A schematic illustration of the cell configuration and the sodium mass-transport process is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b. The removal of Na<sup>+</sup> from the Na-deficient P2-cathode can result in impoverishing Na<sup>+</sup> in the cathode leading to irreversible structural distortion. To solve the irreversibility of the cell, a sacrificial sodium-containing salt has been added to the cathode as a Na<sup>+</sup> supply, according to the literature. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> Our engineering approach by presodiating hard carbon anodes using sodium metal resulted in higher reversible capacity and better capacity retention of 48% for the cell made of P2-cathode and 50% presodiated HC. Most importantly, the cell that comprised the fully sodiated hard carbon (100% DOD) achieved the highest capacity retention of 64%. This sodium-metal-free system helped in mitigating the issue of reaction between the sodium metal and the electrolyte that causes electrolyte decomposition and excessive capacity fade. Indeed, a reversible Na<sup>+</sup> transport can be realized between the fully sodiated hard carbon anode and the Na-deficient P2-Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b). However, even in the presence of HC anodes, which have demonstrated stable cycling performance in SIB half-cells (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c04513/suppl_file/am0c04513_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>), the P2-cathode still shows capacity fading on cycling in the full-cell configuration, which is likely due to other issues such as electrolyte instability, P2-cathode layer collapse, SEI instability, and poor cell design. Previous studies have shown stabilized cycling performance of Mn,Fe-based P2-cathode by introducing stabilizing ion Ti; however, trade-off between lower capacities and stable cycling becomes another concern in SIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> Thus, it is of great interest to develop a simpler process for the presodiation of hard carbon anodes and further stabilizing the cathode for practical SIB manufacturing.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c04513/asset/images/medium/am0c04513_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c04513/asset/images/large/am0c04513_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) Cycling performance of cells made of EA-P2 cathode and hard carbon anode with different presodiation levels; (b) schematic illustration of Na<sup>+</sup> transport mechanism in two different cell configurations with hard carbon anode 0% DOD (pristine) versus 100% DOD (fully sodiated).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c04513/asset/images/large/am0c04513_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c04513&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i10">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In this study, we successfully synthesized P2-Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> by a novel eutectic synthesis approach that yields highly pure materials with improved crystallinity and microstructure for potential application as high-capacity cathodes for SIBs. In comparison with the sol–gel process, we found that the eutectic method holds the promise of improving the Na<sup>+</sup> kinetics at the cathode/electrolyte interface and enhancing the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient. These cathode materials have been coupled with presodiated hard carbon anodes to assess their cycling performances in practical cells. When assembled with fully presodiated hard carbon, the P2-Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode delivered 182 mA h/g reversible capacity and achieved 64% capacity retention over 50 cycles owing to the enhanced structural stability of the cathode ensured by the excess of Na<sup>+</sup> on the anode side. This study sheds light on a novel, scalable, and highly efficient synthesis approach for P2-type Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and provides an engineering approach to maximize cell energy density and performance through presodiation of hard carbon. Looking forward, more practical approaches are needed to secure enough Na<sup>+</sup> inventory at the anode to strengthen the structural stability of P2-type layered cathodes, which may expedite the emergence of practical sodium-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div upon cycling in half-cells, GITT charge/discharge profiles of EA-P2 Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and examples of fitting in order to obtain Na<sup>+</sup>                         cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Role of Alumina Coating on Li-Ni-Co-Mn-O Particles as Positive Electrode Material for Lithium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Myung, Seung-Taek; Izumi, Kentarou; Komaba, Shinichi; Sun, Yang-Kook; Yashiro, Hitoshi; Kumagai, Naoaki</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2005</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">17</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">14</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3695-3704</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CMATEX</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0897-4756</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The interface reaction between Al2O3-coated Li[Li0.05Ni0.4Co0.15Mn0.4]O2 and a liq. electrolyte was studied.  The Al2O3-coated Li[Li0.05Ni0.4Co0.15Mn0.4]O2 showed no large difference in bulk structure, compared to bare Li[Li0.05Ni0.4Co0.15Mn0.4]O2.  The coated Al2O3 has an amorphous structure according to XRD results.  A small amt. of Al2O3 coating (0.25% in the final compn.) showed a uniform mesoporous Al2O3-coating layer whose thickness is ∼5 nm covers Li[Li0.05Ni0.4Co0.15Mn0.4]O2 particles, as confirmed by TEM.  At higher concn. (2.5% in the final compn.), irregular, tens of nanometer-sized Al2O3 powder was obsd. on the surface of the active material, instead of the uniform coating layer.  Despite the insulating nature of Al2O3, the thin coating was effective to improve battery performance, depending on the thickness of the Al2O3-coating layer, and the electrolytic salt used.  The Al2O3 coating resulted in a higher capacity retention, esp. at 60°.  The alumina layer protected against HF attack from the electrolyte during cycling so that the decompn. of active material from HF attack was suppressed.  The lower impedance was ascribed to the pos. effects on the electrode/electrolyte interface, the less amt. of decompn. of active material by HF and/or scavenging of HF by the Al2O3-coating layer into the electrolyte.  These effects made it possible to maintain the morphol. of the active material during extensive cycling.  The bare particles however were severely degraded by cycling, due to HF.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1021/cm980241u"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Manthiram, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Low Temperature Synthesis of Insertion Oxides for Lithium Batteries</span>. <i>Chem.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Springer</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Lithium-ion battery technol. is projected to be the leapfrog technol. for the electrification of the drive-train and to provide stationary storage solns. to enable the effective use of renewable energy sources.  The technol. is already in use for low-power applications such as consumer electronics and power tools.  Extensive research and development has enhanced the technol. to a stage where it seems very likely that safe and reliable lithium-ion batteries will soon be on board hybrid elec. and elec. vehicles and connected to solar cells and windmills.  However, the safety of the technol. is still a concern, service life is not yet sufficient, and costs are too high.  This paper summarizes the state of the art of lithium-ion battery technol. for nonexperts.  It lists materials and processing for batteries and summarizes the costs assocd. with them.  This paper should foster an overall understanding of materials and processing and the need to overcome the remaining barriers for a successful market introduction.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref4" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1021/acs.chemmater.9b02601"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiong, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cui, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Q.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Pillar [5] quinone–Carbon Nanocomposites as High-Capacity Cathodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Chem.                         cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Pillar[5]quinone-Carbon Nanocomposites as High-Capacity Cathodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xiong, Wenxu; Huang, Weiwei; Zhang, Meng; Hu, Pandeng; Cui, Huamin; Zhang, Qichun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">New org. cathodes to replace inorg. materials for the capacity enhancement of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are highly desirable.  In this research, we described the investigation of pillar[5]quinone (P5Q), which we detd. to have a theor. capacity of 446 mAh g-1, a value that makes it a very promising candidate as a cathode in rechargeable batteries.  Inspired by this value, P5Q was encapsulated into CMK-3 to form a composite and then integrated with single-walled carbon nanotubes (SWCNTs) to generate a film that was used as the cathode in SIBs.  The as-assembled SIBs showed an initial capacity up to 418 mAh g-1 and maintained 290 mAh g-1 after 300 cycles at 0.1 C.  Even at 1 C, the capacity could still reach 201 mAh g-1.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref5" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1039/c8ta11994e"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xie, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, Z. Metal org. polymers (MOPs), including metal coordination polymers (CPs, 1-dimensional), metal-org. frameworks (MOFs, 2-/three-dimensional), Prussian blue (PB) and Prussian blue analogs (PBAs), have recently emerged as promising electrochem. active materials for energy storage and conversion systems.  Due to the tunability of their compn. and the structural versatility, diverse electrochem. behaviors for multi-electron reactions, fast-ion diffusion, and small vol. change of electrodes could be achieved upon charging and discharging.  Because of these advantages, MOPs are considered as effective substitutes for future advanced energy storage systems.  Here, the authors summarize the recent progress in pristine MOPs as electrode candidates for rechargeable Li and Na ion batteries.                         cas_cit8R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">8</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Interplay between Electrochemistry and Phase Evolution of the P2-type Nax(Fe1/2Mn1/2)O2 Cathode for Use in Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Pang, Wei Kong; Kalluri, Sujith; Peterson, Vanessa K.; Sharma, Neeraj; Kimpton, Justin; Johannessen, Bernt; Liu, Hua Kun; Dou, Shi Xue; Guo, Zaiping</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">27</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3150-3158</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CMATEX</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries are the next-generation in battery technol.; however, their com. development is hampered by electrode performance.  The P2-type Na2/3(Fe1/2Mn1/2)O2 with a hexagonal structure and P63/mmc space group is considered a candidate sodium-ion battery cathode material due to its high capacity (∼190 mAh·g-1) and energy d. (∼520 mWh·g-1), which are comparable to those of the com. LiFePO4 and LiMn2O4 lithium-ion battery cathodes, with previously unexplained poor cycling performance being the major barrier to its com. application.  The operando synchrotron X-ray powder diffraction is used to understand the origins of the capacity fade of the Na2/3(Fe1/2Mn1/2)O2 material during cycling over the relatively wide 1.5-4.2 V (vs. Na) window.  A complex phase-evolution is found, involving transitions from P63/mmc (P2-type at the open-circuit voltage) to P63 (OP4-type when fully charged) to P63/mmc (P2-type at 3.4-2.0 V) to Cmcm (P2-type at 2.0-1.5 V) symmetry structures during the desodiation and sodiation of the Na2/3(Fe1/2Mn1/2)O2 cathode.  The assocd. large cell-vol. changes with the multiple two-phase reactions are likely to be responsible for the poor cycling performance, clearly suggesting a 2.0-4.0 V window of operation as a strategy to improve cycling performance.  It is demonstrated here that the P2-type Na2/3(Fe1/2Mn1/2)O2 cathode is able to deliver ∼25% better cycling performance with the strategic operation window.  This significant improvement in cycling performance implies that by characterizing the phase evolution and reaction mechanisms during battery function these modifications are proposed to the conditions of battery use that improve performance, highlighting the importance of the interplay between structure and electrochem.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref9" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1021/am502343s"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kalluri, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Seng, K.                         cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrospun P2-type Na2/3(Fe1/2Mn1/2)O2 Hierarchical Nanofibers as Cathode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kalluri, Sujith; Hau Seng, Kuok; Kong Pang, Wei; Guo, Zaiping; Chen, Zhixin; Liu, Hua-Kun; Dou, Shi Xue</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries can be the best alternative to lithium-ion batteries, because of their similar electrochem., nontoxicity, and elemental abundance and the low cost of sodium.  They still stand in need of better cathodes in terms of their structural and electrochem. aspects.  Accordingly, the present study reports the first example of the prepn. of Na2/3(Fe1/2Mn1/2)O2 hierarchical nanofibers by electrospinning.  The nanofibers with aggregated nanocrystallites along the fiber direction have been characterized structurally and electrochem., resulting in enhanced cyclability when compared to nanoparticles, with initial discharge capacity of ∼195 mA-h/g. As promising cathode materials for SIBs, layered transition metal oxides (LTMOs) exhibit high specific capacity and high energy d. due to their appropriate voltage window.                         cas_cit13R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">13</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Apple-Biowaste-Derived Hard Carbon as a Powerful Anode Material for Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wu, Liming; Buchholz, Daniel; Vaalma, Christoph; Giffin, Guinevere A.; Passerini, Stefano</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ChemElectroChem</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Modern industrial agriculture is strongly influenced by product norms and stds., resulting in massive amts. of fresh fruit that is left in the field or wasted in spite of their good nutritional value.  Herein, we present the synthesis of hard carbon from natural apple biowaste, and its use of biomass is a suitable strategy for the development of cheap and powerful carbon-based active materials for Na-ion batteries.  The hard carbon exhibits a good rate capability [112 mAh g-1 at 5 C (1 A g-1)], excellent long-term cycling stability (1000 cycles at 5 C), and high specific capacity (245 mAh g-1 at 0.1 C) with full retention after 80 cycles.  The full capacity (250 mAh g-1) of the hard carbon is also obtained in Na-ion cells by using the layered P2-type NaxNi0.22Co0.11Mn0.66O2 cathode.  The good electrochem. performance as well as the low cost and environmental friendliness of the apple-biowaste-derived hard carbon proves its suitability for future Na-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref14" class="refNumLink">14</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit14" data-doi="10.1002/anie.201505215"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yi, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ishida, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A High-Voltage and Ultralong-Life Sodium Full Cell for Stationary Energy Storage</span>. <i>Angew.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Recently, there has been great interest in developing advanced sodium-ion batteries for large-scale application.  Most efforts have concd. on the search for high-performance electrode materials only in sodium half-cells.  Research on sodium full cells for practical application has encountered many problems, such as insufficient cycles with rapid capacity decay, low safety, and low operating voltage.  Herein, we present a layered P2-Na0.66Ni0.17Co0.17Ti0.66O2, as both an anode (ca. 0.69 V vs. Na+/Na) and as a high-voltage cathode (ca. 3.74 V vs. Na+/Na).         </div><div class="casAbstract">Rechargeable Li batteries have risen to prominence as key devices for green and sustainable energy development.  Mn- and Fe-based cathode materials, such as LiMn2O4 and LiFePO4, are used in large-scale batteries for elec. vehicles.  Mn and Fe are abundant elements in the Earth's crust, but Li is not.  In contrast to Li, Na is an attractive charge carrier from elemental abundance.  Recently, some layered materials, where Na can be electrochem. and reversibly extd./inserted, have been reported.  However, their reversible capacity is typically limited to 100 mA-h/g.  Herein, the authors report a new electrode material, P2-Na2/3[Fe1/2Mn1/2]O2, that delivers 190 mA-h/g of reversible capacity in Na cells with the electrochem. active Fe3+/Fe4+ redox.  These results will contribute to the development of rechargeable batteries from the earth-abundant elements operable at room temp.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref19" class="refNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit19" data-doi="10.1021/cm3029615"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Buchholz, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Moretti, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kloepsch, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nowak, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Siozios, V.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Winter, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Passerini, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Toward Na-ion Batteries-Synthesis and Characterization of a Novel High Capacity Na Ion Intercalation Material</span>. <i>Chem.                         cas_cit19R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">19</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Toward Na-ion Batteries - Synthesis and Characterization of a Novel High Capacity Na Ion Intercalation Material</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Buchholz, Daniel; Moretti, Arianna; Kloepsch, Richard; Nowak, Sascha; Siozios, Vassilios; Winter, Martin; Passerini, Stefano</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The rapid growth of the worldwide demand of lithium for batteries (lithium-ion batteries) can possibly lead to a shortage of its reserves.  Sodium batteries represent a promising alternative because they enable much higher energy densities than other battery systems, with the exception of lithium-ion batteries, and are not limited by sodium availability.  Herein, we present a novel, Na+ ion intercalation material, Na0.45Ni0.22Co0.11Mn0.66O2 (space group P63/mmc) synthesized in air by a copptn. method followed by a thermal treatment and a water-rinsing step.  This material has a sp. capacity of 135 mA-h/g with a Coulombic efficiency &gt;99.7%.  Upon long-term cycling tests the material shows excellent capacity retention after more than 250 cycles.  Such an overall performance, superior to that of presently known sodium-ion cathodes, represents a step further toward the realization of sustainable batteries for efficient stationary energy storage.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref20"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref20" class="refNumLink">20</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit20" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201400083"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hasa, I.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Buchholz, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Passerini, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Scrosati, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hassoun, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High Performance Na0. 5 [Ni0. 23Fe0. 13Mn0. 63] O2 Cathode for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Porous hard carbons are synthesized via carbonizing lotus stems with naturally hierarchical structures.  The hard carbon carbonized at 1400°C (LS1400) delivers a total capacity 350 mAh/g in the c.d. of 100 mA/g and a plateau capacity of 250 mAh/g.  Even cycled at 100 mA/g after 450 cycles, the capacity still retains 94%.  Further investigation shows that the sodium storage of LS carbons involves Na+ adsorption in the defect sites, Na+ insertion and Na metal deposition in the closed pores.  However, the Na metal deposition in closed pores mainly contribute to the plateau capacity, leading to the excellent sodium storage performance of LS1400 with a large closed pore ratio of 66%.  The results show that the intrinsic structure of natural biomass can inspire us to design hard carbon with large closed pore ratio as excellent anode for sodium ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref22"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref22" class="refNumLink">22</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit22" data-doi="10.1039/c3ta01430d"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yuan, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pei, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qian, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ai, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Synthesis and Electrochemical Behaviors of Layered Na-0.67[Mn0.65Co0.2Ni0.15]O-2 Microflakes as a Stable Cathode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Stable Na+ storage cathodes with adequate reversible capacity are needed for enabling Na-ion battery technol. for large scale and low cost elec. storage.  In light of the superior Li+ storage performance of layered oxides, pure P2-phase Na0.67[Mn0.65Ni0.15Co0.2]O2 microflakes are synthesized by a simple sol-gel method and tested as a Na+ storage cathode.                         cas_cit25R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">25</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Synthesis and characterization of high-temperature hexagonal P2-Na0.6MnO2 and its electrochemical behaviour as cathode in sodium cells</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Caballero, A.; Hernan, L.; Morales, J.; Sanchez, L.; Santos Pena, J.; Aranda, M. A. G.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2002</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">12</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A layered sodium manganese oxide, Na0.6MnO2, stable at temps. &gt;800°, was synthesized by using a sol-gel method that employs Mn(acac)3 (ac = acetylacetonate), Na2CO3, and propionic acid to form the resin framework.  This layered bronze possesses a hexagonal, P2-type structure, in which the distortion assocd. with Mn3+ is hardly noticeable.  It reacts slowly, though reversibly, with atm. moisture, which causes the interlayer spacing in the structure to increase by ∼2.5 Å, by intercalation of water mols. into the interlayer gap occupied by Na+ ions.  The anhyd. material was tested as a cathode in sodium cells.  Although the electrochem. intercalation of Na+ occurs in two steps, the host retains its main structural features, with a slight tendency in the interlayer spacing to contract as the sodium content increases.  The similarity between the discharge and charge profiles of the first cycles reveals a quasi-reversible nature of the intercalation process and that the cell can deliver a const. sp. capacity of ∼140 A-h/kg at 0.1 mA/cm2 when cycled in a voltage region of 3.8-2.0 V.  However, the continuous strains and distortions resulting from the insertion and extn. of Na+ ions cause the host structure to gradually collapse and yield an amorphous material after the first eight cycles.  This leads to a progressive redn. of the cell capacity, irresp. of the specific voltage region used.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref26"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref26" class="refNumLink">26</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit26" data-doi="10.1021/acs.chemmater.5b00097"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Duffort, V.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Talaie, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Black, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nazar, L.                         cas_cit26R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">26</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Uptake of CO2 in Layered P2-Na0.67Mn0.5Fe0.5O2: Insertion of Carbonate Anions</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Duffort, Victor; Talaie, Elahe; Black, Robert; Nazar, Linda F.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>
