            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Batteries based on sodium layered transition metal oxides are a promising alternative to current state-of-the-art lithium-ion systems for large-scale energy storage, resulting in recent intensive efforts to develop high-energy d., low cost, stable cathode materials.  Some of the most promising degrade on exposure to ambient atm.; however, the process is not understood.  Here, using neutron/X-ray diffraction coupled with mass spectroscopy and thermal anal., the nature is revealed of the reactivity.  The unprecedented insertion is demonstrated of carbonate ions in the vacancy-rich layered structure of P2-Na0.67[Mn0.5Fe0.5]O2 on exposure to CO2 and moisture, concomitant with oxidn. of Mn(III) to Mn(IV).  The material exhibits much higher charge/discharge polarization and lower capacity than rigorously air-protected P2-Na0.67[Mn0.5Fe0.5]O2; a detailed study by online electrochem. mass spectroscopy reveals that the inserted carbonate ions decomp. during electrochem. charging, accounting for the differences obsd. between the first and second cycles.  Furthermore, it is shown that Ni-substituted materials P2-Na0.67[NixMn0.5+xFe0.5-2x]O2 are less prone to such reactivity and thus are more promising candidates for scalable processing.  Understanding these mechanisms provides a vital guide for future sodium metal oxide battery research.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref27"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref27" class="refNumLink">27</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit27" data-doi="10.1007/s10971-006-1521-7"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hernández, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Maya, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sánchez-Mora, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sánchez, E.                         cas_cit31R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">31</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Exploring Oxygen Activity in the High Energy P2-Type Na0.78Ni0.23Mn0.69O2 Cathode Material for Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ma, Chuze; Alvarado, Judith; Xu, Jing; Clement, Raphaele J.; Kodur, Moses; Tong, Wei; Grey, Clare P.; Meng, Ying Shirley</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Large-scale elec. energy storage is fundamental to the use of renewable energy.  Recently, research and development efforts on room-temp. sodium-ion batteries (NIBs) have been revitalized, as NIBs are considered promising, low-cost alternatives to the current Li-ion battery technol. for large-scale applications.  Herein, we introduce a novel layered oxide cathode material, Na0.78Ni0.23Mn0.69O2.  This new compd. provides a high reversible capacity of 138 mAh g-1 and an av. potential of 3.25 V vs Na+/Na with a single smooth voltage profile.  Its remarkable rate and cycling performances are attributed to the elimination of the P2-O2 phase transition upon cycling to 4.5 V.  The first charge process yields an abnormally excess capacity, which has yet to be obsd. in other P2 layered oxides.  Metal K-edge XANES results show that the major charge compensation at the metal site during Na-ion deintercalation is achieved via the oxidn. of nickel (Ni2+) ions, whereas, to a large extent, manganese (Mn) ions remain in their Mn4+ state.  Interestingly, electron energy loss spectroscopy (EELS) and soft x-ray absorption spectroscopy (sXAS) results reveal differences in electronic structures in the bulk and at the surface of electrochem. cycled particles.  At the surface, transition metal ions (TM ions) are in a lower valence state than in the bulk, and the O K-edge prepeak disappears.  On the basis of previous reports on related Li-excess LIB cathodes, it is proposed that part of the charge compensation mechanism during the first cycle takes place at the lattice oxygen site, resulting in a surface to bulk transition metal gradient.  We believe that by optimizing and controlling oxygen activity, Na layered oxide materials with higher capacities can be designed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref32"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref32" class="refNumLink">32</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit32" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.5b11848"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bai, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, F.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Enhanced Sodium Ion Storage Behavior of P2-Type Na2/3Fe1/2Mn1/2O2 Synthesized via a Chelating Agent Assisted Route</span>. <i>ACS Appl.         </div><div class="casAbstract">From resource abundance and low cost, high capacity layered P2-type Na2/3Fe1/2Mn1/2O2 material is regarded as a potential cathode material for Na-ion batteries but suffers from its unstable structure during cycling.  P2-type Na2/3Fe1/2Mn1/2O2 layered materials were synthesized by a chelating agent assisted sol-gel method with NH3·H2O.  With the addn. of NH3·H2O and the control of the synthesis conditions, highly active material with a more stable structure and better electrochem. performance was obtained.  Also, the influences of structure changes during different voltage ranges (1.5-4.0 V and 1.5-4.3 V vs. Na+/Na) on the Na+ storage behavior was also evaluated and compared.  When being charged to 4.2 V, an OP4-type phase emerges, which can reduce the damage by the gilding of the MeO2 layers but leads to an unstable crystal structure.  For long-term cycling, it is preferred to cut off at 4.0 V rather than at 4.3 V.  For the optimized P2-type Na2/3Fe1/2Mn1/2O2 calcined at 900°, a discharge capacity of 92 mA h/g remains after 40 cycles in the voltage range of 1.5-4.0 V, and the Coulombic efficiency remains 100%.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref33"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref33" class="refNumLink">33</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit33" data-doi="10.1002/cssc.201901056"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pfeifer, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Arnold, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Becherer, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Das, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Maibach, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ehrenberg, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dsoke, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Can Metallic Sodium Electrodes Affect the Electrochemistry of Sodium-Ion Batteries?         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries (NIBs) are promising energy-storage devices with advantages such as low cost and highly abundant raw materials.  To probe the electrochem. properties of NIBs, Na metal is most frequently applied as the ref. and/or counter electrode in state-of-the-art literature.  However, the high reactivity of the Na metal and its impact on the electrochem. performance is usually neglected.  Spontaneous reactions of Na metal with org. electrolytes and the importance of crit. interpretation of electrochem. expts. is emphasized.  When using Na-metal half-cells, decompn. products contaminate the electrolyte during the electrochem. measurement and can easily lead to wrong conclusions about the stability of the active materials.  The cycling stability is highly affected by these electrolyte contaminations, which is proven by comparing Na-metal-free cell with Na-metal-contg. cells.  A more stable cycling performance of the Li4Ti5O12 half-cells can be obsd. when replacing the Na metal counter and ref. electrodes with activated C electrodes.  This difference is attributed to the altered properties of the electrolyte as a result of contamination and to different surface chemistries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref34"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref34" class="refNumLink">34</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit34" data-doi="10.1039/c4ee00465e"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Billaud, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Singh, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Armstrong, A.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Additive-free pellets of Li1-xNi0.5Mn1.5O4 have been prepd. for the purpose of performing ionic diffusivity and exchange c.d. studies.  Here we report on the characterization of interfacial charge transfer kinetics and ionic diffusivity of ordered (P4332) and disordered (Fd‾3m) Li1-xNi0.5Mn1.5O4 as a function of lithium content at ambient temp.  The exchange c.d. at the electrode/electrolyte interface is found to be continuously increased with increasing the degree of delithiation for ordered phase (∼0.21-6.5 mA/cm2) at (x = 0.01-0.60), in contrast the disordered phase exhibits gradually decrease of exchange c.d. in the initial delithiation at the 4 V plateau regime (x = 0.01-0.04) and again monotonously increases (0.65-6.8 mA/cm2) with further delithiation at (x = 0.04-0.60).  The ionic diffusivity of ordered and disordered phase is found to be ∼5 × 10-10cm2s-1 and ∼10-9cm2s-1, resp., and does not vary much with the degree of delithiation.  From the obtained results it appears that the chem. diffusivity during electrochem. use is limited by lithium transport, but is fast enough over the entire state-of-charge range to allow charge/discharge of micron-scale particles at practical C-rates.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref36"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref36" class="refNumLink">36</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit36" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2017.02.071"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Amin, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Belharouk, I.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Part I: Electronic and Ionic Transport Properties of the Ordered and Disordered LiNi0. 5Mn1. 5O4 Spinel Cathode</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit36R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">36</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Part I: Electronic and ionic transport properties of the ordered and disordered LiNi0.5Mn1.5O4 spinel cathode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Amin, Ruhul; Belharouk, Ilias</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Here, we report on the electronic and ionic cond. and diffusivity of the ordered (P4332) and disordered (Fd‾3m) LiNi0.5Mn1.5O4 spinel material, which have been detd. by using ion and electron blocking cell configurations as a function of lithium concn. and temp.  The disordered phase exhibits about fifteen-time higher electronic cond. than the ordered phase at room temp. in the lithiated state.  Upon delithiation, the electronic cond. of the ordered LiNi0.5Mn1.5O4 phase increases and reaches the same levels obsd. for the disordered phase.  The ionic cond. and diffusivity of LiNi0.5Mn1.5O4, in the ordered and disordered forms, are in the range of ∼1 × 10-9 S/cm and ∼5 × 10-9 cm2/s, resp.  Both phases exhibit similar activation energies for the ionic cond. and diffusivity, i.e. 0.70 ± 0.2eV and 0.74 ± 0.2eV, resp.  It can be concluded from the obtained results that the electrochem. performance of LiNi0.5Mn1.5O4, whether ordered or disordered, is limited by lithium transport, but is fast enough to allow charge/discharge of micron-scale particles at practical C-rates.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref37"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref37" class="refNumLink">37</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit37" data-doi="10.1149/2.0131608jes"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Amin, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chiang, Y.-M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Characterization of Electronic and Ionic Transport in Li1-xNi0. 33Mn0. 33Co0. 33O2 (NMC333) and Li1-xNi0. 50Mn0. 20Co0. 30O2 (NMC523) as a Function of Li Content</span>. <i>J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Recently, Na-ion batteries (SIBs) have been considered as the promising alternative for Li-ion batteries.         (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">247</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">377-383</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPSODZ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0378-7753</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. performance of Na0.45Ni0.22Co0.11Mn0.66O2 in an ionic liq.-based electrolytic solns. is reported and compared with that obtained in a conventional, carbonate-based electrolyte.  Even at ambient temp., the Na-ion intercalation material reveals a much better electrochem. performance in 10 mol% NaTFSI (or 0.45 M) in PYR14FSI electrolyte than 0.5M NaPF6 in PC electrolyte in terms of specific capacity and cycling stability.  In particular, the electrodes cycled in the IL-based electrolyte combine a capacity retention of ∼80% after 100 cycles with high specific capacities (∼200 mA-h g-1) and high av. voltage (2.7 V vs. Na/Na+), demonstrating that Na0.45Ni0.22Co0.11Mn0.66O2 is a promising cathode material for Na-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref43"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref43" class="refNumLink">43</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit43" data-doi="10.1021/am5080437"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hasa, I.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Buchholz, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Passerini, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hassoun, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Comparative Study of Layered Transition Metal Oxide Cathodes for Application in Sodium-Ion Battery</span>. <i>ACS Appl.                         cas_cit43R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">43</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Comparative study of layered transition metal oxide cathodes for application in sodium-ion battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hasa, Ivana; Buchholz, Daniel; Passerini, Stefano; Hassoun, Jusef</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">9</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5206-5212</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AAMICK</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1944-8244</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Herein, we report a study on P-type layered sodium transition metal-based oxides with a general formula of NaxMO2 (M = Ni, Fe, Mn).  We synthesize the materials via copptn. followed by annealing in air and rinsing with water, and we examine the electrodes as cathodes for sodium-ion batteries using a propylene carbonate-based electrolyte.  We fully investigate the effect of the Ni-to-Fe ratio, annealing temp., and sodium content on the electrochem. performances of the electrodes.  The impact of these parameters on the structural and electrochem. properties of the materials is revealed by X-ray diffraction, SEM, and cyclic voltammetry, resp.  The suitability of this class of P-type materials for sodium battery application is finally demonstrated by cycling tests revealing an excellent electrochem. performance in terms of delivered capacity (i.e., about 200 mAh g-1) and charge-discharge efficiency (approaching 100%).</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref44"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref44" class="refNumLink">44</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit44" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2019.134693"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">De Ilarduya, J.                 10.1021/acsami.8b18488</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D11%26publication_year%3D2019%26pages%3D5957-5965%26journal%3DACS%2BAppl.%2BMater.%2BInterfaces%26author%3DJ.%2BH.%2BJo%26author%3DJ.%2BU.%2BChoi%26author%3DY.%2BJ.%2BPark%26author%3DJ.%2BZhu%26author%3DH.%2BYashiro%26author%3DS.-T.%2BMyung%26title%3DNew%2BInsight%2Binto%2BEthylenediaminetetraacetic%2BAcid%2BTetrasodium%2BSalt%2Bas%2Ba%2BSacrificing%2BSodium%2BIon%2BSource%2Bfor%2BSodium-Deficient%2BCathode%2BMaterials%2Bfor%2BFull%2BCells%26doi%3D10.1021%252Facsami.8b18488&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.0c04513&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facsami.8b18488&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit45R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">45</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">New Insight into Ethylenediaminetetraacetic Acid Tetrasodium Salt as a Sacrificing Sodium Ion Source for Sodium-Deficient Cathode Materials for Full Cells</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jo, Jae Hyeon; Choi, Ji Ung; Park, Yun Ji; Zhu, Jiefang; Yashiro, Hitoshi; Myung, Seung-Taek</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">11</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5957-5965</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AAMICK</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1944-8244</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sacrificing sodium supply sources is needed for sodium-deficient cathode materials to achieve commercialization of sodium-ion full cells using sodium-ion intercalation anode materials.  Herein, the potential of EDTA tetrasodium salt (EDTA-4Na) as a sacrificing sodium supply source was investigated by intimately blending it with sodium-deficient P2-type Na0.67[Al0.05Mn0.95]O2.  The EDTA-4Na/Na0.67[Al0.05Mn0.95]O2 composite electrode unexpectedly exhibited an improved charge capacity of 177 mA h (g-oxide)-1 compared with the low charge capacity of 83 mA h (g-oxide)-1 for bare Na0.67[Al0.05Mn0.95]O2.  The reversible capacity of an EDTA-4Na/Na0.67[Al0.05Mn0.95]O2//hard carbon full-cell system increased to 152 mA h (g-oxide)-1 at the first discharge with a Coulombic efficiency of 89%, whereas the Na0.67[Al0.05Mn0.95]O2 without EDTA-4Na delivered a discharge capacity 51 mA h g-1 because of the small charge capacity.  The EDTA-4Na sacrificed itself to generate Na+ ions via oxidative decompn. by releasing four sodium ions and producing C3N as a decompn. resultant on charge.  It is thought that the slight increase in discharge capacity is assocd. with the electroconducting nature of the C3N deposits formed on the surface of the Na0.67[Al0.05Mn0.95]O2 electrode.  We elucidated the reaction mechanism and sacrificial activity of EDTA-4Na, and our findings suggest that the addn. of EDTA-4Na is beneficial as an addnl. source of Na+ ions that contribute to the charge capacity.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref46"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref46" class="refNumLink">46</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit46" data-doi="10.1021/acs.chemmater.5b03276"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Han, M. M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Armand, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Avdeev, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Saiz Garitaonandia, J. J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rojo, T.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High-Performance P2-Phase Na2/3Mn0.8Fe0.1Ti0.1O2 Cathode Material for Ambient-Temperature Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Chem. Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>28</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">106</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">116</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/acs.chemmater.5b03276</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D28%26publication_year%3D2016%26pages%3D106-116%26journal%3DChem.%2BMater.%26author%3DM.%2BH.%2BHan%26author%3DE.%2BGonzalo%26author%3DN.%2BSharma%26author%3DJ.%2BM.%2BL%25C3%25B3pez%2Bdel%2BAmo%26author%3DM.%2BArmand%26author%3DM.%2BAvdeev%26author%3DJ.%2BJ.%2BSaiz%2BGaritaonandia%26author%3DT.%2BRojo%26title%3DHigh-Performance%2BP2-Phase%2BNa2%252F3Mn0.8Fe0.1Ti0.1O2%2BCathode%2BMaterial%2Bfor%2BAmbient-Temperature%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1021%252Facs.chemmater.5b03276&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.0c04513&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facs.chemmater.5b03276&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit46R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">46</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High-Performance P2-Phase Na2/3Mn0.8Fe0.1Ti0.1O2 Cathode Material for Ambient-Temperature Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Han, Man Huon; Gonzalo, Elena; Sharma, Neeraj; Lopez del Amo, Juan Miguel; Armand, Michel; Avdeev, Maxim; Saiz Garitaonandia, Jose Javier; Rojo, Teofilo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">28</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">106-116</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CMATEX</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0897-4756</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">High-performance Mn-rich P2-phase Na2/3Mn0.8Fe0.1Ti0.1O2 is synthesized by a ceramic method, and its stable electrochem. performance is demonstrated. 23Na solid-state NMR confirms the substitution of Ti4+ ions in the transition metal oxide layer and very fast Na+ mobility in the interlayer space.  The pristine electrode delivers a second charge/discharge capacity of 146.57/144.16 mA·h·g-1 and retains 95.09% of discharge capacity at the 50th cycle within the voltage range 4.0-2.0 V at C/10.  At 1C, the reversible specific capacity still reaches 99.40 mA·h·g-1, and capacity retention of 87.70% is achieved from second to 300th cycle.  In addn., the moisture-exposed electrode reaches reversible capacities of more than 130 and 80 mA·h·g-1 for C/10 and 1C, resp., with excellent capacity retention.  The correlation between overall electrochem. performance of both electrodes and crystal structural characteristics are investigated by neutron powder diffraction.  The stability of pristine electrode's crystallog. structure during the charge/discharge process has been investigated by in situ X-ray diffraction, where only a solid soln. reaction occurs within the given voltage range except for a small biphasic mechanism occurring at or below 2.2 V during the discharge process.  The relatively small substitution (20%) at the transition metal site leads to stable electrochem. performance, which is in part derived from the structural stability during electrochem. cycling.  Therefore, the small cosubstitution (e.g., with Ti and Fe) route suggests a possible new scope for the design of sodium-ion battery electrodes that are suitable for long-term cycling.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></div><script type="text/javascript" nonce="9318800ecba2075b-SJC">window.refViewer={'ref1':['cit1'],'ref2':['cit2'],'ref3':['cit3'],'ref4':['cit4'],'ref5':['cit5'],'ref6':['cit6'],'ref7':['cit7'],'ref8':['cit8'],'ref9':['cit9'],'ref10':['cit10'],'ref11':['cit11'],'ref12':['cit12'],'ref13':['cit13'],'ref14':['cit14'],'ref15':['cit15'],'ref16':['cit16'],'ref17':['cit17'],'ref18':['cit18'],'ref19':['cit19'],'ref20':['cit20'],'ref21':['cit21'],'ref22':['cit22'],'ref23':['cit23'],'ref24':['cit24'],'ref25':['cit25'],'ref26':['cit26'],'ref27':['cit27'],'ref28':['cit28'],'ref29':['cit29'],'ref30':['cit30'],'ref31':['cit31'],'ref32':['cit32'],'ref33':['cit33'],'ref34':['cit34'],'ref35':['cit35'],'ref36':['cit36'],'ref37':['cit37'],'ref38':['cit38'],'ref39':['cit39'],'ref40':['cit40'],'ref41':['cit41'],'ref42':['cit42'],'ref43':['cit43'],'ref44':['cit44'],'ref45':['cit45'],'ref46':['cit46']}</script><div class="articleCitedByDropzone" data-pb-dropzone="articleCitedByDropzone"><div class="cited-by"><div class="article_content-header cited-content_header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div tabindex="0" class="article_content-title"><h2 id="citeThis">Cited By</h2><div class="article__copy"> <button class="article__copy__btn"><i aria-hidden="true" class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><div class="cited-by__content"><div class="sciteMetricsDropzone">

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The interface reaction between Al2O3-coated Li[Li0.05Ni0.4Co0.15Mn0.4]O2 and a liq. electrolyte was studied.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries are the next-generation in battery technol.; however, their com. development is hampered by electrode performance.  The P2-type Na2/3(Fe1/2Mn1/2)O2 with a hexagonal structure and P63/mmc space group is considered a candidate sodium-ion battery cathode material due to its high capacity (∼190 mAh·g-1) and energy d. (∼520 mWh·g-1), which are comparable to those of the com. LiFePO4 and LiMn2O4 lithium-ion battery cathodes, with previously unexplained poor cycling performance being the major barrier to its com. application.  The operando synchrotron X-ray powder diffraction is used to understand the origins of the capacity fade of the Na2/3(Fe1/2Mn1/2)O2 material during cycling over the relatively wide 1.5-4.2 V (vs. Na) window.  A complex phase-evolution is found, involving transitions from P63/mmc (P2-type at the open-circuit voltage) to P63 (OP4-type when fully charged) to P63/mmc (P2-type at 3.4-2.0 V) to Cmcm (P2-type at 2.0-1.5 V) symmetry structures during the desodiation and sodiation of the Na2/3(Fe1/2Mn1/2)O2 cathode.  The assocd. large cell-vol. changes with the multiple two-phase reactions are likely to be responsible for the poor cycling performance, clearly suggesting a 2.0-4.0 V window of operation as a strategy to improve cycling performance.  It is demonstrated here that the P2-type Na2/3(Fe1/2Mn1/2)O2 cathode is able to deliver ∼25% better cycling performance with the strategic operation window.  This significant improvement in cycling performance implies that by characterizing the phase evolution and reaction mechanisms during battery function these modifications are proposed to the conditions of battery use that improve performance, highlighting the importance of the interplay between structure and electrochem.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref9" class="rightTabRefNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit9" data-doi="10.1021/am502343s"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kalluri, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Seng, K.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The rapid growth of the worldwide demand of lithium for batteries (lithium-ion batteries) can possibly lead to a shortage of its reserves.  Sodium batteries represent a promising alternative because they enable much higher energy densities than other battery systems, with the exception of lithium-ion batteries, and are not limited by sodium availability.  Herein, we present a novel, Na+ ion intercalation material, Na0.45Ni0.22Co0.11Mn0.66O2 (space group P63/mmc) synthesized in air by a copptn. method followed by a thermal treatment and a water-rinsing step.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Porous hard carbons are synthesized via carbonizing lotus stems with naturally hierarchical structures.  The hard carbon carbonized at 1400°C (LS1400) delivers a total capacity 350 mAh/g in the c.d. of 100 mA/g and a plateau capacity of 250 mAh/g.  Even cycled at 100 mA/g after 450 cycles, the capacity still retains 94%.  Further investigation shows that the sodium storage of LS carbons involves Na+ adsorption in the defect sites, Na+ insertion and Na metal deposition in the closed pores.  However, the Na metal deposition in closed pores mainly contribute to the plateau capacity, leading to the excellent sodium storage performance of LS1400 with a large closed pore ratio of 66%.  The results show that the intrinsic structure of natural biomass can inspire us to design hard carbon with large closed pore ratio as excellent anode for sodium ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref22"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref22" class="rightTabRefNumLink">22</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit22" data-doi="10.1039/c3ta01430d"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yuan, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pei, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qian, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ai, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Synthesis and Electrochemical Behaviors of Layered Na-0.67[Mn0.65Co0.2Ni0.15]O-2 Microflakes as a Stable Cathode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Batteries based on sodium layered transition metal oxides are a promising alternative to current state-of-the-art lithium-ion systems for large-scale energy storage, resulting in recent intensive efforts to develop high-energy d., low cost, stable cathode materials.  Some of the most promising degrade on exposure to ambient atm.; however, the process is not understood.  Here, using neutron/X-ray diffraction coupled with mass spectroscopy and thermal anal., the nature is revealed of the reactivity.  The unprecedented insertion is demonstrated of carbonate ions in the vacancy-rich layered structure of P2-Na0.67[Mn0.5Fe0.5]O2 on exposure to CO2 and moisture, concomitant with oxidn. of Mn(III) to Mn(IV).  The material exhibits much higher charge/discharge polarization and lower capacity than rigorously air-protected P2-Na0.67[Mn0.5Fe0.5]O2; a detailed study by online electrochem. mass spectroscopy reveals that the inserted carbonate ions decomp. during electrochem. charging, accounting for the differences obsd. between the first and second cycles.  Furthermore, it is shown that Ni-substituted materials P2-Na0.67[NixMn0.5+xFe0.5-2x]O2 are less prone to such reactivity and thus are more promising candidates for scalable processing.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Large-scale elec. energy storage is fundamental to the use of renewable energy.  Recently, research and development efforts on room-temp. sodium-ion batteries (NIBs) have been revitalized, as NIBs are considered promising, low-cost alternatives to the current Li-ion battery technol. for large-scale applications.  Herein, we introduce a novel layered oxide cathode material, Na0.78Ni0.23Mn0.69O2.  This new compd. provides a high reversible capacity of 138 mAh g-1 and an av. potential of 3.25 V vs Na+/Na with a single smooth voltage profile.  Its remarkable rate and cycling performances are attributed to the elimination of the P2-O2 phase transition upon cycling to 4.5 V.  The first charge process yields an abnormally excess capacity, which has yet to be obsd. in other P2 layered oxides.  Metal K-edge XANES results show that the major charge compensation at the metal site during Na-ion deintercalation is achieved via the oxidn. of nickel (Ni2+) ions, whereas, to a large extent, manganese (Mn) ions remain in their Mn4+ state.  Interestingly, electron energy loss spectroscopy (EELS) and soft x-ray absorption spectroscopy (sXAS) results reveal differences in electronic structures in the bulk and at the surface of electrochem. cycled particles.  At the surface, transition metal ions (TM ions) are in a lower valence state than in the bulk, and the O K-edge prepeak disappears.  On the basis of previous reports on related Li-excess LIB cathodes, it is proposed that part of the charge compensation mechanism during the first cycle takes place at the lattice oxygen site, resulting in a surface to bulk transition metal gradient.  We believe that by optimizing and controlling oxygen activity, Na layered oxide materials with higher capacities can be designed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref32"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref32" class="rightTabRefNumLink">32</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit32" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.5b11848"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bai, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, F.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Enhanced Sodium Ion Storage Behavior of P2-Type Na2/3Fe1/2Mn1/2O2 Synthesized via a Chelating Agent Assisted Route</span>. <i>ACS Appl.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries (NIBs) are promising energy-storage devices with advantages such as low cost and highly abundant raw materials.  To probe the electrochem. properties of NIBs, Na metal is most frequently applied as the ref. and/or counter electrode in state-of-the-art literature.  However, the high reactivity of the Na metal and its impact on the electrochem. performance is usually neglected.  Spontaneous reactions of Na metal with org. electrolytes and the importance of crit. interpretation of electrochem. expts. is emphasized.  When using Na-metal half-cells, decompn. products contaminate the electrolyte during the electrochem. measurement and can easily lead to wrong conclusions about the stability of the active materials. A more stable cycling performance of the Li4Ti5O12 half-cells can be obsd. when replacing the Na metal counter and ref. electrodes with activated C electrodes.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Additive-free pellets of Li1-xNi0.5Mn1.5O4 have been prepd. for the purpose of performing ionic diffusivity and exchange c.d. studies. The exchange c.d. at the electrode/electrolyte interface is found to be continuously increased with increasing the degree of delithiation for ordered phase (∼0.21-6.5 mA/cm2) at (x = 0.01-0.60), in contrast the disordered phase exhibits gradually decrease of exchange c.d. in the initial delithiation at the 4 V plateau regime (x = 0.01-0.04) and again monotonously increases (0.65-6.8 mA/cm2) with further delithiation at (x = 0.04-0.60). We fully investigate the effect of the Ni-to-Fe ratio, annealing temp., and sodium content on the electrochem. performances of the electrodes.  The impact of these parameters on the structural and electrochem. properties of the materials is revealed by X-ray diffraction, SEM, and cyclic voltammetry, resp.  The suitability of this class of P-type materials for sodium battery application is finally demonstrated by cycling tests revealing an excellent electrochem. performance in terms of delivered capacity (i.e., about 200 mAh g-1) and charge-discharge efficiency (approaching 100%).</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref44"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref44" class="rightTabRefNumLink">44</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit44" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2019.134693"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">De Ilarduya, J. M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Otaegui, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Galceran, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Acebo, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shanmukaraj, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rojo, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Armand, M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Towards High Energy Density, Low Cost and Safe Na-Ion Full-Cell Using P2-Na-0.67[Fe0.5Mn0.5]O-2 and Na2C4O4 Sacrificial Salt</span>. <i>Electrochim. Acta</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>321</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">134693</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.electacta.2019.134693</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord empty" id="
                            rightTab-cas_R
                        "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref45"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref45" class="rightTabRefNumLink">45</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit45" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.8b18488"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jo, J. H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Choi, J. U.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Park, Y. J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yashiro, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Myung, S.-T.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">New Insight into Ethylenediaminetetraacetic Acid Tetrasodium Salt as a Sacrificing Sodium Ion Source for Sodium-Deficient Cathode Materials for Full Cells</span>. <i>ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>11</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">5957</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">5965</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/acsami.8b18488</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit45R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">45</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">New Insight into Ethylenediaminetetraacetic Acid Tetrasodium Salt as a Sacrificing Sodium Ion Source for Sodium-Deficient Cathode Materials for Full Cells</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jo, Jae Hyeon; Choi, Ji Ung; Park, Yun Ji; Zhu, Jiefang; Yashiro, Hitoshi; Myung, Seung-Taek</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">11</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5957-5965</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AAMICK</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1944-8244</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sacrificing sodium supply sources is needed for sodium-deficient cathode materials to achieve commercialization of sodium-ion full cells using sodium-ion intercalation anode materials.  Herein, the potential of EDTA tetrasodium salt (EDTA-4Na) as a sacrificing sodium supply source was investigated by intimately blending it with sodium-deficient P2-type Na0.67[Al0.05Mn0.95]O2.  The EDTA-4Na/Na0.67[Al0.05Mn0.95]O2 composite electrode unexpectedly exhibited an improved charge capacity of 177 mA h (g-oxide)-1 compared with the low charge capacity of 83 mA h (g-oxide)-1 for bare Na0.67[Al0.05Mn0.95]O2.  The reversible capacity of an EDTA-4Na/Na0.67[Al0.05Mn0.95]O2//hard carbon full-cell system increased to 152 mA h (g-oxide)-1 at the first discharge with a Coulombic efficiency of 89%, whereas the Na0.67[Al0.05Mn0.95]O2 without EDTA-4Na delivered a discharge capacity 51 mA h g-1 because of the small charge capacity.  The EDTA-4Na sacrificed itself to generate Na+ ions via oxidative decompn. by releasing four sodium ions and producing C3N as a decompn. resultant on charge.  It is thought that the slight increase in discharge capacity is assocd. with the electroconducting nature of the C3N deposits formed on the surface of the Na0.67[Al0.05Mn0.95]O2 electrode.  We elucidated the reaction mechanism and sacrificial activity of EDTA-4Na, and our findings suggest that the addn. of EDTA-4Na is beneficial as an addnl. source of Na+ ions that contribute to the charge capacity.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref46"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref46" class="rightTabRefNumLink">46</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit46" data-doi="10.1021/acs.chemmater.5b03276"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Han, M. H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gonzalo, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sharma, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">López del Amo, J. M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Armand, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Avdeev, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Saiz Garitaonandia, J. J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rojo, T.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High-Performance P2-Phase Na2/3Mn0.8Fe0.1Ti0.1O2 Cathode Material for Ambient-Temperature Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Chem. Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>28</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">106</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">116</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/acs.chemmater.5b03276</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit46R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">46</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High-Performance P2-Phase Na2/3Mn0.8Fe0.1Ti0.1O2 Cathode Material for Ambient-Temperature Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Han, Man Huon; Gonzalo, Elena; Sharma, Neeraj; Lopez del Amo, Juan Miguel; Armand, Michel; Avdeev, Maxim; Saiz Garitaonandia, Jose Javier; Rojo, Teofilo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">28</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">106-116</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CMATEX</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0897-4756</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">High-performance Mn-rich P2-phase Na2/3Mn0.8Fe0.1Ti0.1O2 is synthesized by a ceramic method, and its stable electrochem. performance is demonstrated. 23Na solid-state NMR confirms the substitution of Ti4+ ions in the transition metal oxide layer and very fast Na+ mobility in the interlayer space.  The pristine electrode delivers a second charge/discharge capacity of 146.57/144.16 mA·h·g-1 and retains 95.09% of discharge capacity at the 50th cycle within the voltage range 4.0-2.0 V at C/10.  At 1C, the reversible specific capacity still reaches 99.40 mA·h·g-1, and capacity retention of 87.70% is achieved from second to 300th cycle.  In addn., the moisture-exposed electrode reaches reversible capacities of more than 130 and 80 mA·h·g-1 for C/10 and 1C, resp., with excellent capacity retention.  The correlation between overall electrochem. performance of both electrodes and crystal structural characteristics are investigated by neutron powder diffraction.  The stability of pristine electrode's crystallog. structure during the charge/discharge process has been investigated by in situ X-ray diffraction, where only a solid soln. reaction occurs within the given voltage range except for a small biphasic mechanism occurring at or below 2.2 V during the discharge process.  The relatively small substitution (20%) at the transition metal site leads to stable electrochem. performance, which is in part derived from the structural stability during electrochem. cycling.  Therefore, the small cosubstitution (e.g., with Ti and Fe) route suggests a possible new scope for the design of sodium-ion battery electrodes that are suitable for long-term cycling.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li><li id="pane-m3" aria-labelledby="pane-m3" class="tab__pane tab__pane-suppInfo"><h2 class="supp-info-label">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article_supporting-info" id="rightTab-notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i11">Supporting Information</h2></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.0c04513?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.0c04513</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">XRD profile matching refinement results for SG-P2 and EA-P2 Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, voltage curves of SG-P2 and EA-P2 indicating differences in polarization and Coulombic efficiency values of SG-P2 and EA-P2 upon cycling in half-cells, GITT charge/discharge profiles of EA-P2 Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and examples of fitting in order to obtain Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient values, Nyquist plot and EIS fitting based on the equivalent circuit of EA-P2 Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of a hard carbon anode against Na metal, SEM images of SG-P2, EA-P2 Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> in half-cells and EA-P2 Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> in full cells after cycling and elemental ratio from EDS results, charge/discharge and d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> curves for the 50th cycle of SG-P2, EA-P2 in half-cells, and EA-P2 in full-cells, rate performance of SG-P2 and EA-P2 in SIB half-cells, and cycling performance of hard carbon anode in SIB half-cells (<a 	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {

	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }

	});

<script nonce="9318800ecba2075b-SJC">
document.addEventListener('DOMContentLoaded', function () {
    const modalContainer = document.querySelector('.my-main-container');

    // Function to show the modal
    function showModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'flex';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'block';
    }

    // Function to hide the modal when the close button is clicked
    function hideModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'none';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'none';
    }

    // Hide the modal initially
    hideModal();

    // Function to handle clicks outside the modal
    function handleClickOutside(event) {
        // const modalContainer = document.querySelector(".my-main-container");
        // Check if the click target is not a child of the modal container
        if (!modalContainer.contains(event.target)) {
            hideModal();
        }
    }

    // Add a click event listener to the close button
    document
        .querySelector('.close-button')
        .addEventListener('click', hideModal);

    // Add a click event listener to the document to handle clicks outside the modal
    document.addEventListener('click', handleClickOutside);

    const screenHeight = window.innerHeight;
    function updateModalPosition() {
        const scrollY = window.scrollY;
        // console.log("scrollY= " + scrollY);
        // modalContainer.style.top = `${50 + scrollY}px`;
        modalContainer.style.top = `${scrollY + screenHeight / 2}px`;
        // console.log("new top= " + modalContainer.style.top);
    }

    // Update the modal position when the user scrolls
    window.addEventListener('scroll', updateModalPosition);

    // Initialize the modal position
    updateModalPosition();

    //---Cookie---
    // Function to set the cookie with a given name, value, and expiration time in days
    function setCookie(name, value, days) {
        var expires = '';
        if (days) {
            var date = new Date();
            date.setTime(date.getTime() + days * 24 * 60 * 60 * 1000);
            expires = '; expires=' + date.toUTCString();
            console.log(expires + 'this is expires dateUTC');
        }
        document.cookie = name + '=' + (value || '') + expires + '; path=/';
        // console.log("document.cookie is: " + document.cookie);
    }

    if (!document.cookie.includes('modalAd')) {
        // Show Modal
        showModal();

        // Set the cookie with the name "modalAd", value "true", and expiration of 3 day
        setCookie('modalAd', 'true', 3);
    } else {
        hideModal();
    }
});

</script>

<script nonce="9318800ecba2075b-SJC">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

        time = 3000,
        btnStatus = true,
        errorStatus = false,

          "<p class='gdpr_err'><b>Please Enter a valid email address<b><p>";

      function init() {
        setTimeout(function () {
          var cookieExist = getCookie("gdpr");
          if (cookieExist) {
            //console.log("gdpr cookie exist");
            return false;
          } else {
            //console.log("gdpr cookie does not exist")
            gdprMessage();
          }
          //Events
          $("#closebtn").on("click", hideForm);

          //Handle submit
          $("#gdpr-message-btn").on("click", function (event) {
            handleClick();
          });

          // //Hide if clicked outside div - TO DO stop handling click after submit
          // $(document).click(function(event) {
          // 	event.stopPropagation();
          //   console.log(event.target);
          //   if (!$(event.target).closest("#gdpr-message-content").length) {
          //     $("body").find("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
          //     $(this).off();
          //   }
          // });
          $("#gdpr-con-btn").click(function () {
            setCookie("gdpr", "true");
            hideForm();
          });
        }, time);
      }

      //Show the message
      function gdprMessage() {
        //console.log("Show GDPR Message");
        var tMessage, emailInput, messageButton, ewrap;

        $("#gdpr-message-modal").show();
      }

      function handleClick() {
        //console.log($("#gdpr-input").val());
        emailValid = validateEmail($("#gdpr-input").val());
        if (emailValid) {
          //Submit the form
          //Hide form
          if ($(".gdpr_err")) {
            $(".gdpr_err").hide();
          }

          $("#gdpr-message-content");
          //If successful then...
          setCookie("gdpr", "true");
          //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid + " submiting form");
        } else {
          //Show error message
          gdprErrorMessage();
          //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid);
        }
      }

      function gdprErrorMessage() {
        if (errorStatus) {
          return false;
        } else {
          errorStatus = true;
          $("#gdpr-message-content").append(errormessage);
        }
      }

      function validateEmail(email) {
        var re =
          /^(([^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+(\.[^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+)*)|(".+"))@((\[[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\])|(([a-zA-Z\-0-9]+\.)+[a-zA-Z]{2,}))$/;
        return re.test(String(email).toLowerCase());
      }

      function setCookie(name, value) {
        var expires = "";
        expires = "; expires=Fri, 31 Dec 9999 23:59:59 GMT";
        document.cookie = name + "=" + (value || "") + expires + "; path=/";
      }

      function getCookie(name) {
        var match = document.cookie.match(new RegExp(name + "=([^;]+)"));
        if (match) return match[1];
      }

      function hideForm() {
        //And set cookie
        $("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
        setCookie("gdpr", "false");
        gtag("event", "GDPR Modal", {
          event_category: "Pubs GDPR",
          event_label: "GDPR Dismissal",
        });
      }

      return {
        init: init,
      };
    })();

  </script>
</p>

var currentJournal = "ACS Applied Materials &amp;amp; Interfaces";
$(".citationLinks > a").click(function(e){
    if($(this).text() === "ACS Full Text ") {
        gtag('event', 'ACS Full Text', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Crossref") {
        gtag('event', 'Crossref', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "CAS") {
        gtag('event', 'CAS', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "PubMed") {
        gtag('event', 'PubMed', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Google Scholar") {
        gtag('event', 'Google Scholar', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).hasClass("issue-item_open-url") === true) {
        gtag('event', 'OpenURL', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
});
</script>
